Besides, the conormal bundle $\Nu _ { C / X } ^ { * }$ has degree $- \mathbf { K } _ { X } ^ { 2 }$ ωwhich is positive by assumption, so that com/bining Riemann-Roch and Serre duality [29, p. 82–83], we get $\mathbf { h } ^ { 0 } ( C , \mathbf { N } _ { C / X } ^ { * } ) = - \mathbf { K } _ { X } ^ { 2 }$ and $ { \mathbf { h } } ^ { 1 } ( C , { \mathbf { N } } _ { C / X } ^ { \* } )$ vanishes.
Therefore $\mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( C , \Omega _ { _ { X | C } } ^ { 1 } ) \simeq \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( C , \Omega _ { C } ^ { 1 } )$ .
It follows that $\pi$ / /can be identified with the pullback morphism from $\mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( X , \Omega _ { X } ^ { 1 } )$ to $\mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( C , \Omega _ { C } ^ { 1 } )$ induced by the injection of the curve $C$ in $X$ .
We have two ,exact sequences of right $\langle f \rangle$ ,-modules

$$
\left\{ \begin{array} { l l } { 0 \longrightarrow \mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( C , \Omega _ { X | C } ^ { 1 } ) \longrightarrow \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( X , \mathrm { T } X ) ^ { * } \longrightarrow \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 , 1 } ( X ) \longrightarrow \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( C , \Omega _ { C } ^ { 1 } ) \longrightarrow 0 } \\ { 0 \longrightarrow \mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( C , \mathrm { N } _ { C / X } ^ { * } ) \longrightarrow \mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( C , \Omega _ { X | C } ^ { 1 } ) \longrightarrow \mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( C , \Omega _ { C } ^ { 1 } ) \longrightarrow 0 } \end{array} \right.
$$

If $C$ is smooth, this yields $Q _ { f } ( x ) \left( x - 1 \right) = P _ { f } ( x ) \theta _ { C } ( x ) \left( x - a _ { f } \right)$ If $C$ is cuspidal, Lemma 3.10 gives

$$
Q _ { f } ( x ) ( x - 1 ) ( x - a _ { f } ^ { - 1 } ) = P _ { f } ( x ) \theta _ { C } ( x ) ( x - a _ { f } ^ { - 5 } ) ( x - a _ { f } ^ { - 7 } ) .
$$

3.2.3. Cycle of rational curves.
We now investigate the case of effective anticanonical divisors given by a cycle of smooth rational curves and leave the three other exceptional configurations to the reader.

Remark that if $f$ is an automorphism of a rational surface with positive entropy, then $\vert - \mathrm { K } _ { X } \vert$ is either empty or consists of a single divisor (otherwise $f$ would preserve a rational fibration).
In this last case, by replacing $f$ by an iterate, we can assume that all irreducible components of the divisor are globally invariant by $f$ .

Theorem 3.13. Let X be a basic rational surface without nonzero holomorphic vector field.
Assume that there exists a reducible and reduced effective anticanonical cycle $\begin{array} { r } { D = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { r } D _ { i } } \end{array}$ on $X$ such that $D _ { i } ^ { 2 } < 0$ for all i.
For any automorphism $f$ of $X$ leaving each $D _ { i }$ globally invariant, let $P _ { f }$ and $\theta _ { i , f }$ <denote the characteristic polynomials of $f ^ { * }$ acting on ${ \mathrm { N S } } _ { \mathbb { Q } } ( X )$ , and $\mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( D _ { i } , \mathrm { N } _ { D _ { i } / X } ^ { * } ( - S _ { i } ) )$ θ , respectively, where $S _ { i } = \operatorname { s i n g } ( D ) \cap D _ { i }$ .
Lastly, let $Q _ { f }$ be the characteristic polynomial of $f _ { * }$ /acting on $\mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( X , \mathrm { T } X )$ .
Then

$$
Q _ { f } ( x ) = \frac { P _ { f } ( x ) } { ( x - 1 ) ^ { r } } \prod _ { i = 1 } ^ { r } \theta _ { i , f } ( x ) .
$$

Proof.
Let $S$ be the singular locus of $D$ .
Then we have an exact sequence

$$
0 \longrightarrow \Omega _ { X | D } ^ { 1 } \longrightarrow \oplus _ { i = 1 } ^ { r } \Omega _ { X | D _ { i } } ^ { 1 } \longrightarrow \Omega _ { X | S } ^ { 1 } \longrightarrow 0 .
$$

Since $\mathrm { N } _ { D _ { i } / X } ^ { * }$ has positive degree, $\mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( D _ { i } , \Omega _ { X | D _ { i } } ^ { 1 } ) \simeq \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( D _ { i } , \Omega _ { D _ { i } } ^ { 1 } ) \simeq { \mathbb C }$ .
Besides, as $\mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( D _ { i } , \Omega _ { D _ { i } } ^ { 1 } ) = 0$ , we /obtain the isomorphism $\mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( D _ { i } , \Omega _ { \boldsymbol { X } | D _ { i } } ^ { 1 } ) \simeq \mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( \bar { D } _ { i } , \mathrm { N } _ { D _ { i } / X } ^ { * } )$ ,.
It follows that

$$
\mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( D , \Omega _ { X | D } ^ { 1 } ) \simeq \bigoplus _ { i = 1 } ^ { r } \mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( D , \mathrm { N } _ { D _ { i } / X } ^ { * } ( - S _ { i } ) )
$$

and that the map from $\textstyle \bigoplus _ { i = 1 } ^ { r } \operatorname { H } ^ { 0 } ( D _ { i } , \Omega _ { X | D _ { i } } ^ { 1 } )$ to $\mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( S , \Omega _ { X | S } ^ { 1 } )$ is onto.
Therefore

$$
\mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( D , \Omega _ { X | D } ^ { 1 } ) \simeq \bigoplus _ { i = 1 } ^ { r } \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } \left( D _ { i } , \Omega _ { X | D _ { i } } ^ { 1 } \right) \simeq \bigoplus _ { i = 1 } ^ { r } \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( D _ { i } , \Omega _ { D _ { i } } ^ { 1 } ) \simeq { \mathbb C } ^ { r } .
$$

We now write down the exact sequence (3.4) used in the proof of Theorem 3.11:

$$
0 \longrightarrow \bigoplus _ { i = 1 } ^ { r } \mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( D , \mathrm { N } _ { D _ { i } / X } ^ { * } ( - S _ { i } ) ) \longrightarrow \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( X , \mathrm { T } X ) ^ { * } \longrightarrow \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 , 1 } ( X ) \longrightarrow \mathbb { C } ^ { r } \longrightarrow \mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( X , \mathrm { T } X ) ^ { * } \longrightarrow 0 \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 / 2 } \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( X ) \longrightarrow \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 / 2 } \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 / 2 } \longrightarrow 0 .
$$

Since we assumed that there were no nonzero holomorphic vector fields on $X$ , we get

$$
Q _ { f } ( x ) \left( x - 1 \right) ^ { r } = P _ { f } ( x ) \prod _ { i = 1 } ^ { r } \theta _ { i , f } ( x ) .
$$

3.3. Examples.
We have chosen three main examples of application of Theorem 3.11, but this doesn’t exhaust the list of possibilities.
For instance, other cases of quadratic Cremona transformations leaving a cubic invariant can be dealt with, e.g. [14, Ex. 3.3 and Th. 4.4].

3.3.1. Quadratic transformations fixing a cuspidal cubic.
We start by recalling the construction of automorphisms of rational surfaces of positive entropy obtained from quadratic transformations of the projective plane fixing a cuspidal cubic.
This construction goes back to McMullen [40], but we will follow [14].

Let $\mathcal { C }$ be the cuspidal cubic in $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ given in homogeneous coordinates $( x : y : z )$ by the equation $y ^ { 2 } z = x ^ { 3 }$ , and let $P _ { 0 } = ( 0 : 1 : 0 )$ .
The stabilizer in $\mathrm { P G L } ( 3 ; \mathbb { C } )$ of the cubic $\mathcal { C }$ is isomorphic to $\mathbb { C } ^ { \times }$ , each parameter $\delta$ in $\mathbb { C } ^ { \times }$ corresponding to the linear transformation $( x : y : z ) \to ( \delta x : y : \delta ^ { 3 } z )$ .
There δis a natural algebraic group structure on ${ \mathcal { C } } ^ { \mathrm { r e g } }$ with $P _ { 0 }$ δ δas origin compatible with the group structure on ${ \mathrm { P i c } } ^ { 0 } ( { \mathcal { C } } )$ , and the isomorphism $t \to ( t : 1 : t ^ { 3 } )$ between $\mathbb { C }$ and ${ \mathcal { C } } ^ { \mathrm { r e g } }$ is an isomorphism of algebraic groups (see [30, Ex. 6.11.4]).

Let $\tau$ be a permutation of the set {1 2 3}, and let $n _ { 1 } , n _ { 2 } , n _ { 3 }$ be three integers.
The set $\{ \tau , n _ { 1 } , n _ { 2 } , n _ { 3 } \}$ τis called an orbit data (see [3]).
If $| \tau |$ ,is the order of $\tau$ in the symmetric group $\mathfrak { S } _ { 3 }$ τ, , ,, we define two polynomials $p _ { \tau }$ and $P _ { \tau }$ as follows:

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \left\{ \begin{array} { l l } { p _ { \tau } ( x ) = 1 - 2 x + \sum _ { j = \tau ( j ) } x ^ { 1 + n _ { j } } + \sum _ { j \neq \tau ( j ) } x ^ { n _ { j } } ( 1 - x ) } \\ { P _ { \tau } ( x ) = x ^ { 1 + n _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } + n _ { 3 } } p _ { \tau } ( x ^ { - 1 } ) + ( - 1 ) ^ { | \tau | } p _ { \tau } ( x ) . } \end{array} \right. } \end{array}
$$

The degree of $P _ { \tau }$ is $n _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } + n _ { 3 } + 1$ , and $P _ { \tau } ^ { * } = P _ { \tau }$ .
Let $f$ be a quadratic birational transformation of $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ τ, and assume that the base locus of $f$ τ τconsists of three distinct points $p _ { 1 } ^ { + } , p _ { 2 } ^ { + }$ and ${ p } _ { 3 } ^ { + }$ .
If $\Delta _ { i j }$ denotes the line between $p _ { i } ^ { + }$ and $\boldsymbol { p } _ { j } ^ { + }$ , then the base locus of $f ^ { - 1 }$ consists of the three points $\qquad p _ { 1 } ^ { - } = f ( \Delta _ { 2 3 } )$ , $p _ { 2 } ^ { - } = f ( \Delta _ { 1 3 } )$ and $p _ { 3 } ^ { - } = f ( \Delta _ { 1 2 } )$ .
We say that $f$ realizes the orbit data if for any $j$ with $1 \leq j \leq 3$ , the orbit $\{ f ^ { k } ( p _ { j } ^ { - } ) \} _ { 0 \leq k < n _ { j } - 1 }$ consists of $n _ { j }$ pairwise distinct points outside the base loci of $f$ and $f ^ { - 1 }$ and if $f ^ { n _ { j } - 1 } ( p _ { j } ^ { - } ) = p _ { \tau ( j ) } ^ { + }$ <.
In this case, the birational map $f$ lifts to an automorphism of the rational surface τobtained by blowing up the $n _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } + n _ { 3 }$ points corresponding to the orbits of the points $\boldsymbol { p } _ { j } ^ { - }$ .
The corresponding characteristic polynomial for the action on the Picard group of this surface is $P _ { \tau }$ (see [14, $\ S 2 . 1 ] $ ).
We say that an orbit data is admissible if it satisfies the following conditions:

$$
\left\{ \begin{array} { l l } { \operatorname { I f } n _ { 1 } = n _ { 2 } = n _ { 3 } \ { \mathrm { t h e n } } \ \tau = { \mathrm { i d } } . } \\ { \operatorname { I f } n _ { i } = n _ { j } \ { \mathrm { f o r } } \ i \neq j \ { \mathrm { e i t h e r } } \ \tau ( i ) \neq j \ { \mathrm { o r } } \ \tau ( j ) \neq i . } \\ { \operatorname { A l l } \ n _ { i } \ { \mathrm { a r e } } \ { \mathrm { a t } } \ l { \mathrm { e a s t } } \ 3 \ { \mathrm { a n d } } \ { \mathrm { o n e ~ i s ~ a t ~ l e a s t } } \ 4 . } \end{array} \right.
$$

Proposition 3.14 ([14, Thm. 1 & 3]).

(i) Let $p _ { 1 } ^ { + }$ , $p _ { 2 } ^ { + }$ and ${ p } _ { 3 } ^ { + }$ be three points in $\mathcal { C } ^ { \mathrm { r e g } }$ such that $p _ { 1 } ^ { + } + p _ { 2 } ^ { + } + p _ { 3 } ^ { + } \neq 0$ , and let $\mu$ be a given number in $\mathbb { C } ^ { \times }$ .
Then there exists a unique quadratic transformation $f$ of $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ µfixing $\mathcal { C }$ , having $p _ { 1 } ^ { + } , \ p _ { 2 } ^ { + }$ and ${ p } _ { 3 } ^ { + }$ as base points, and such that $f _ { | \mathcal { C } }$ has multiplier $\mu$ .
Besides, the translation factor of $f _ { | \mathcal { C } }$ is $\epsilon = { \textstyle { \frac { 1 } { 3 } } } ( p _ { 1 } ^ { + } + p _ { 2 } ^ { + } + p _ { 3 } ^ { + } ) \mu$ and the points $\boldsymbol { p } _ { j } ^ { - }$ µare given by $p _ { j } ^ { - } = \mu p _ { j } ^ { + } - 2 \epsilon$ for $1 \leq j \leq 3$ .

(ii) Let $( \tau , n _ { 1 } , n _ { 2 } , n _ { 3 } )$ be an admissible orbit data, and assume furthermore that $\mu$ is a root of $P$ τ, , , that is not a root of unity.
Then there exist $p _ { 1 } ^ { + } , \ p _ { 2 } ^ { + }$ and ${ p } _ { 3 } ^ { + }$ with $p _ { 1 } ^ { + } + p _ { 2 } ^ { + } + p _ { 3 } ^ { + } \neq 0$ such that the quadratic map $f$ given in $( i )$ realizes the orbit data $( \tau , n _ { 1 } , n _ { 2 } , n _ { 3 } )$ and has multiplier $\mu$ τ, , ,.
Besides, such a quadratic map is unique modulo conjugation by the centralizer of $\mathcal { C }$ in $\mathrm { P G L } ( 3 ; \mathbb { C } )$ .

Proposition 3.15. Let $p _ { 1 } ^ { + }$ , $p _ { 2 } ^ { + }$ and ${ p } _ { 3 } ^ { + }$ be three points in ${ \mathcal { C } } ^ { \mathrm { r e g } }$ such that $p _ { 1 } ^ { + } + p _ { 2 } ^ { + } + p _ { 3 } ^ { + } \neq 0 $ , let $( \tau , n _ { 1 } , n _ { 2 } , n _ { 3 } )$ be an admissible orbit data, let $\mu$ be a root of $P _ { \tau }$ that is not a root of unity, and let $f$ be τ, , , µ τthe corresponding birational quadratic map given by Proposition 3.14. Then the eigenvalues of df at the unique fixed point on $\mathcal { C } ^ { r e g }$ are $\mu$ and $\mu ^ { 3 - n _ { 1 } - n _ { 2 } - n _ { 3 } }$ .

Proof.
Due to a lack of conceptual arguments, we provide a direct proof by calculation, which is computer assisted.
We sketch the argument and refer to the Maple file for computational details.

Since the stabilizer of $\mathcal { C }$ in $\mathrm { P G L } ( 3 ; \mathbb { C } )$ acts transitively on $\mathcal { C } ^ { \mathrm { r e g } }$ , we can assume without loss of generality that $p _ { 1 } ^ { + } = ( 1 : 1 : 1 )$ .
We put $p _ { 2 } ^ { + } = ( \alpha : 1 : \alpha ^ { 3 } )$ and $p _ { 3 } ^ { + } = ( \beta : 1 : \beta ^ { 3 } )$ and assume that $\alpha + \beta \neq - 1$ .
If $\sigma : \mathbb { P } ^ { 2 } \ \xrightarrow { } \mathbb { - } \ \mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ α αis the Cremona involution given by $\sigma ( x : y : z ) = ( y z : x z : x y )$ and $N$ α β σis the linear transformation sending the three points $( 1 : 0 : 0 )$ , $( 0 : 1 : 0 )$ and $( 0 : 0 : 1 )$ to $p _ { 1 } ^ { + } , p _ { 2 } ^ { + }$ and ${ p } _ { 3 } ^ { + }$ respectively, then the base locus of the quadratic transformation $\sigma \circ N$ consists of the three points $\{ p _ { i } ^ { + } \} _ { 1 \leq i \leq 3 }$ .
It is then possible to find a matrix $M$ such that $f = M \circ \sigma \circ N$ fixes the cuspidal cubic $\mathcal { C }$ .
Up to multiplying by an element of the centralizer of $\mathcal { C }$ in $\mathrm { P G L } ( 3 ; \mathbb { C } )$ , we can arrange that the multiplier of $f _ { | \mathcal { C } }$ is $\mu$ .

The translation factor is $\begin{array} { r } { \epsilon = \frac { 1 } { 3 } ( \alpha + \beta + 1 ) \mu } \end{array}$ , so that the unique point on $\mathcal { C } ^ { \mathrm { r e g } }$ fixed by $f$ is $\begin{array} { r } { p = \frac { ( \alpha + \beta + 1 ) \mu } { 3 ( 1 - \mu ) } } \end{array}$ · We have $\begin{array} { r } { p _ { 1 } ^ { - } = \frac { 1 } { 3 } ( 1 - 2 \alpha - 2 \beta ) \mu } \end{array}$ , $\begin{array} { r } { p _ { 2 } ^ { - } = \frac { 1 } { 3 } ( - 2 + \alpha - 2 \beta ) \mu } \end{array}$ and $\begin{array} { r } { p _ { 3 } ^ { - } = \frac { 1 } { 3 } ( - 2 - 2 \alpha + \beta ) \mu } \end{array}$ µ.
We now write α β µdown the conditions in order that $f$ α β µrealizes the orbit data.
We have $f ^ { n } ( t ) = \mu ^ { n } ( t - p ) + p$ , so that we must solve the equations

$$
\mu ^ { n _ { i } - 1 } ( p _ { i } ^ { - } - p ) + p = p _ { i } ^ { + } \quad \mathrm { f o r } 1 \leq i \leq 3 .
$$

If we take two of these three equations, we have two affine equations in the variables $\alpha$ and $\beta$ that give uniquely $\alpha$ and $\beta$ as rational fractions in $\mu$ α β.
The third equation is automatically satisfied if $\mu$ is a root of $P _ { \tau }$ α β µ.
Then we can compute the eigenvalues of $\mathrm { d } f$ at $p$ : the multiplier $\mu$ µis of course an eigenvalue, and the other one $\zeta$ is a rational fraction in $\mu$ µ.
Now an explicit calculation yields the formula $\zeta \mu ^ { n _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } + n _ { 3 } - 3 } = 1$ .


Theorem 3.16. Let $( \tau , n _ { 1 } , n _ { 2 } , n _ { 3 } )$ be an admissible orbit data, let $\mu$ be a root of $P _ { \tau }$ that is not a root of unity, and let $f$ τ, , , µ be a birational quadratic map realizing the orbit data $( \tau , n _ { 1 } , n _ { 2 } , n _ { 3 } ) .$ , fixing the cuspidal cubic $\mathcal { C }$ , and having multiplier $\mu$ when restricted to the cubic $\mathcal { C }$ .
If $X$ , , is the corresponding rational surface and if g is the lift of $f$ µ as an automorphism of $X ,$ , then $\operatorname { m } ( X , g ) \leq 3 - | \tau |$ .
In particular, $i f \tau$ has order three, then $g$ is rigid.

Proof.
Recall that $X$ is obtained by blowing up $n _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } + n _ { 3 }$ points, and that the conormal bundle of the strict transform of $\mathcal { C }$ in $X$ has degree $n _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } + n _ { 3 } - 9$ .
Thanks to Theorem 3.11 (a), Corollary 3.9 (ii) and Proposition 3.15, the characteristic polynomial $Q _ { g }$ of $g$ acting on $\mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( X , \mathrm { T } X )$ is given by

$$
Q _ { g } ( x ) = { \frac { P _ { \tau } ( x ) \left( \prod _ { j = 6 } ^ { n _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } + n _ { 3 } - 3 } ( x - \mu ^ { - j } ) \right) ( x - \mu ^ { - 5 } ) ( x - \mu ^ { - 7 } ) } { ( x - 1 ) ( x - \mu ^ { - 1 } ) } } = { \frac { P _ { \tau } ( x ) \prod _ { j = 5 } ^ { n _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } + n _ { 3 } - 3 } ( x - \mu ^ { - j } ) } { ( x - 1 ) ( x - \mu ^ { - 1 } ) } } .
$$

We look at the multiplicity of the root 1 in $Q _ { g }$ .
Since $\mu$ is not a root of unity, we can reduce the problem to the polynomial $P _ { \tau }$ µ.
We claim that 1 occurs with multiplicity $4 - | \tau |$ .
We deal with the three different cases.

– If $\tau = ( 1 ) ( 2 ) ( 3 )$ , $P _ { \tau } ( 1 ) = P _ { \tau } ^ { \prime } ( 1 ) = P _ { \tau } ^ { ( 2 ) } ( 1 ) = 0$ , and $\begin{array} { r } { P _ { \tau } ^ { ( 3 ) } ( 1 ) = 6 n _ { 1 } n _ { 2 } n _ { 3 } \left( \frac { 1 } { n _ { 1 } } + \frac { 1 } { n _ { 2 } } + \frac { 1 } { n _ { 3 } } - 1 \right) < 0 . } \end{array}$  
– If $\tau = ( 1 2 ) ( 3 )$ , $P _ { \tau } ( 1 ) = P _ { \tau } ^ { \prime } ( 1 ) = 0$ , and $P _ { \tau } ^ { ( 2 ) } ( 1 ) = 2 [ 4 n _ { 3 } - ( n _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } ) ( n _ { 3 } - 1 ) ] \neq 0$ .
Now $n _ { 3 } - 1$ τcannot divide $4 n _ { 3 }$ τexcept if $n _ { 3 } = 3$ or $n _ { 3 } = 5$ .
If $n _ { 3 } = 3$ , we would have $n _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } = 6$ , which is not possible.
If $n _ { 3 } = 5$ , we get $n _ { 1 } + n _ { 2 } = 5$ which is also excluded.  
$- \ \mathrm { I f } \ \tau = ( 1 2 3 ) , P _ { \tau } ( 1 ) = 0$ , and $P _ { \tau } ^ { \prime } ( 1 ) = 9 - n _ { 1 } - n _ { 2 } - n _ { 3 } < 0 .$

3.3.2. The construction of Blanc and Gizatullin.
We start by a brief recollection of the construction of automorphisms fixing a smooth elliptic curve in [5].

Let $\mathcal { C }$ be a smooth cubic in $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ .
For any point $p$ in $\mathcal { C }$ , let $\sigma _ { p }$ be the birational involution of $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ defined as follows: if $\ell$ is a generic line of $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ passing through $p$ σ, $( \sigma _ { p } ) _ { | \ell }$ is the involution of $\ell$ fixing the two \`other intersection points of $\ell$ with $\mathcal { C }$ .
The involution $\sigma _ { p }$ σ \` \`has five distinct 7 base points (including $p \mathrm { , }$ \` σ), and becomes an automorphism on the rational surface obtained by blowing up these five points.
We denote the set of five base points of $\sigma _ { p }$ by $S _ { p }$ .
If $S _ { p } = \{ p , p _ { 1 } , p _ { 2 } , p _ { 3 } , p _ { 4 } \}$ , then the $p _ { i }$ ’s are the four points of $\mathcal { C }$ such that $( p p _ { i } )$ σ is tangent to $\mathcal { C }$ at $p _ { i }$ .
Let $E _ { p } , E _ { p _ { 1 } } , E _ { p _ { 2 } } , E _ { p _ { 3 } } , E _ { p _ { 4 } }$ be the exceptional divisors of the blowup.
The lift of $\sigma _ { p }$ maps $E _ { p }$ , ,to the unique conic of $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ ,passing through all points of $S _ { p }$ and each $E _ { p _ { i } }$ to the line $( p p _ { i } )$ .
Thus the action of $\sigma _ { p }$ on the Picard group of the blown-up surface is given by

$$
\left\{ \begin{array} { l l } { E _ { p } \to 2 H - E _ { p } - E _ { p _ { 1 } } - E _ { p _ { 2 } } - E _ { p _ { 3 } } - E _ { p _ { 4 } } } \\ { E _ { p _ { i } } \to H - E _ { p } - E _ { p _ { i } } \quad 1 \leq i \leq 4 } \\ { H \to 3 H - 2 E _ { p } - E _ { p _ { 1 } } - E _ { p _ { 2 } } - E _ { p _ { 3 } } - E _ { p _ { 4 } } } \end{array} \right.
$$

where $H$ is the pull-back of the hyperplane class (see [5, Lemma 17]).

Let us now fix three points $p , q , r$ on $\mathcal { C }$ such that the set $S _ { p } , S _ { q }$ and $S _ { r }$ do not overlap (the construction works with arbitrary many points and without the genericity condition, see [5] for further details).
Then $\sigma _ { p } , \sigma _ { q }$ and $\sigma _ { r }$ lift to automorphisms of the rational surface $X$ obtained by blowing up $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ σ σin the 15 points $S _ { p } , S _ { q } ,$ $S _ { r }$ (we will still denote the lifts by $\sigma _ { p } , \sigma _ { q }$ and $\sigma _ { r }$ ) and provides an embedding of the free product $\mathbb { Z } / 2 \mathbb { Z } \star \mathbb { Z } / 2 \mathbb { Z } \star \mathbb { Z } / 2 \mathbb { Z }$ in Aut $( X )$ .

This construction can be made in families: the family of smooth cubics in $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ with three (ordered) distinct marked points is a smooth quasi-projective variety $\boldsymbol { \mathbf { \mathit { \Phi } } } _ { \mathbf { \mathit { \Phi } } } \mathbf { \mathcal { \mathbf { \Lambda } } } _ { \mathbf { \mathcal { V } } }$ of dimension $9 + 3 = 1 2$ .
We have a natural deformation $\mathfrak { T }$ of rational surfaces over $_ \mathrm { ~ \textit ~ { ~ N ~ } ~ }$ : for any point $( C , p , q , r )$ in $_ \textmd { ‰}$ , the corresponding rational surface $X$ is the blowup of the projective plane $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ at $S _ { p } , S _ { q }$ , ,and $S _ { r }$ .
Besides, $\mathfrak { T }$ is endowed with three involutions ${ \mathfrak { s } } _ { p } , { \mathfrak { s } } _ { q }$ and ${ \mathfrak { s } } _ { r }$ .

Let $\Psi = \mathfrak { s } _ { p } \circ \mathfrak { s } _ { q } \circ \mathfrak { s } _ { r }$ ; it is an automorphism of $\mathfrak { T }$ .
For any $\nu$ in $_ \mathrm { ~ \textit ~ { ~ N ~ } ~ }$ , the characteristic polynomial of $\Psi _ { \nu } ^ { * }$ acting on the Picard group of ${ \mathfrak { T } } _ { \nu }$ is $P _ { \Psi _ { \nu } } = ( t ^ { 2 } - 1 8 t + 1 ) ( t - 1 ) ^ { 4 } ( t + 1 ) ^ { 1 0 }$ so that the first dynamical degree of $\Psi _ { \nu }$ is $9 + 4 \sqrt { 5 }$ .
In particular $\Psi _ { \nu }$ has positive entropy.

Theorem 3.17. The family $( \mathfrak { T } , \Psi )$ is complete at all of its fibers.

Proof.
The three involutions $\sigma _ { p } , \sigma _ { q }$ and $\sigma _ { r }$ fix pointwise the strict transform $C$ of the curve $\mathcal { C }$ , and act by multiplication by $^ { - 1 }$ σ σ σ on any fiber of the conormal bundle $\Nu _ { C / X } ^ { * }$ .
Thus for any point $\nu$ in the base $_ \mathrm { ~ \textit ~ { ~ N ~ } ~ }$ , $\Theta _ { \Psi _ { \nu } } = ( t + 1 ) ^ { 6 }$ and $a _ { \Psi _ { \nu } } = 1$ /.
Hence we get by Theorem 3.11 the formula

$$
Q _ { \Psi _ { \nu } } = ( t ^ { 2 } - 1 8 t + 1 ) ( t - 1 ) ^ { 4 } ( t + 1 ) ^ { 1 6 }
$$

so that dim ker $( \Psi _ { \nu } ^ { * } - \mathrm { i d } ) = 4$ .
The base $_ \mathrm { ~ \textit ~ { ~ N ~ } ~ }$ of $\mathfrak { T }$ is a submanifold of $S _ { 1 5 } ^ { \dagger }$ of dimension 12 which is $\mathrm { P G L } ( 3 ; \mathbb { C } )$ -invariant.
By Proposition 3.1, the Kodaira-Spencer map of $( \mathfrak { T } , \Psi )$ has rank 4 at every point.
It is therefore surjective, and the result follows from Theorem 2.7. 

3.3.3. Unnodal Halphen surfaces.
We start with a short reminder about Halphen surfaces.
We refer to [10, Prop. 2.1] and [24, $\ S 7 ]$ for more details.

Definition 3.18. A Halphen surface of index $m$ is a rational surface $X$ such that $\vert - m \mathbf { K } _ { X } \vert$ has no fixed part and defines a base point free pencil.

If $X$ is a Halphen surface of index $m$ , ${ \mathrm K } _ { X } ^ { 2 } = 0$ and the generic fiber of the pencil $- m \mathbf { K } _ { X }$ is a smooth elliptic curve.
In fact, Halphen surfaces are exactly the minimal rational surfaces.
They are obtained by blowing up a pencil of curves of degree $3 m$ in $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ with 9 base points 8 along the base locus of the pencil, and $\vert - m \mathbf { K } _ { X } \vert$ is the strict transform of the pencil.
If $m = 1$ , all members of the pencil are anticanonical divisors.
If $m \geq 2$ , the Riemann-Roch theorem yields the existence of an anticanonical divisor $\mathfrak { D }$ , and the only multiple fiber of the elliptic pencil is $m { \mathfrak { D } }$ .
Besides, $\Nu _ { \mathfrak { D } / X } ^ { * }$ is a torsion point of index $m$ in $\operatorname { P i c } ( { \mathfrak { D } } )$ .

Let us explain a concrete way (given at the end of $[ 1 0 , \ S 2 . 2 ]$ , see also [33, $\ S 1 0 . 5 . 1 ] $ ) to construct Halphen surfaces directly without Halphen pencils.
For simplicity, we will only consider the case where the anticanonical divisor is smooth and reduced.
Let $\mathcal { C }$ be a smooth cubic in $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ , and consider nine pairwise distinct points $^ 9 p _ { 1 } , \ldots , p _ { 9 }$ on $\mathcal { C }$ .
Let h be the class $O _ { \mathbb { P } ^ { 2 } } ( 1 ) _ { | \mathcal { C } }$ in $\operatorname { P i c } ( { \mathcal { C } } )$ .
We define $X$ as the blowup of $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ , .
.at the nine points $p _ { i }$ .
Let $\begin{array} { r } { \mathfrak { d } = 3 \mathfrak { h } - \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { 9 } [ p _ { i } ] . } \end{array}$ , and let $C$ be the strict transform of $\mathcal { C }$ in $X$ .
Considering d as a divisor on $C$ , $\mathfrak { d } = \mathrm { N } _ { C / X } ^ { * }$ in $\operatorname { P i c } ^ { 0 } ( C )$ .
If $m \mathfrak { d } = 0$ , then $X$ is a Halphen surface of index $m$ .
Let $o$ be an inflexion point of $\mathcal { C }$ /, so that $\mathrm { ~ b ~ } \sim \ 3 o$ .
Choosing $o$ as an origin in $\mathcal { C }$ and denoting by $\oplus$ the group law in $\mathcal { C }$ , the condition $m \mathfrak { d } = 0$ in $\operatorname { P i c } ^ { 0 } ( C )$ means that $m ( p _ { 1 } \oplus . . . \oplus p _ { 9 } ) = o$ in $\mathcal { C }$ .

We can put this construction in families.
We must distinguish the cases $m = 1$ and $m \ge 2$ , which behave slightly differently.

If $m \geq 2$ , let ${ \mathcal { U } } _ { m }$ be the set of pairs $\{ \mathcal { C } , p _ { 1 } , . . . , p _ { 9 } \}$ where $\mathcal { C }$ is a smooth cubic, the $p _ { i }$ ’s lie on $\mathcal { C }$ and are pairwise distinct, and $m \mathfrak { d } = 0$ in ${ \mathrm { P i c } } ^ { 0 } ( { \mathcal { C } } )$ .
,; this is a smooth quasi-projective variety of dimension $9 + 9 - 1 = 1 7$ with a natural action of $\mathrm { P G L } ( 3 ; \mathbb { C } )$ .
Besides, we have a universal family ${ \mathfrak { H } } _ { m }$ of Halphen surfaces of index $m$ over ${ \mathcal { U } } _ { m }$ .
Since Halphen surfaces of index $m \ge 2$ have a unique anticanonical divisor, the cubic $\mathcal { C }$ is entirely determined by the points $p _ { i }$ , so that we can see ${ \mathcal { U } } _ { m }$ as a locally closed smooth algebraic variety in the configuration space $( { \mathbb { P } } ^ { 2 } ) ^ { 9 } / { \mathfrak { S } } _ { 9 }$ stable by the action of $\mathrm { P G L } ( 3 ; \mathbb { C } )$ .

If $m = 1$ , the nine points $p _ { i }$ don’t determine the cubic curve $\mathcal { C }$ .
Let $\mathcal { U } _ { 1 }$ denote the set of pencils of cubics on $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ with smooth generic fiber and no infinitely near points in the base locus, which is a Zariski open subset of $\mathbb { P } ^ { 1 6 }$ that can be seen as a smooth locally closed subset of $( { \mathbb { P } } ^ { 2 } ) ^ { 9 } / { \mathfrak { S } } _ { 9 }$ via the map associating to each pencil its base locus.
The variety $\mathcal { U } _ { 1 }$ carries a natural family ${ \mathfrak { H } } _ { 1 }$ /of Halphen surfaces of index 1 obtained by blowing up the base locus of the pencils.
Besides, $\mathcal { U } _ { 1 }$ is again stable by the action of $\mathrm { P G L } ( 3 ; \mathbb { C } )$ .

Lastly, let us recall some results about unnodal Halphen surfaces.
We refer the reader to [10, $\ S 2 . 3 ]$ for more details.
A Halphen surface is called unnodal if all members of the pencil $\vert - m \mathbf { K } _ { X } \vert$ are irreducible.
This condition is generic among Halphen surfaces of index $m$ .
In particular, every genericity condition we have assumed on the Halphen set is satisfied for unnodal Halphen surfaces.
Besides, their automorphisms group admits a particularly nice description: if $X$ is unnodal, the lattice $\mathfrak { L } ( X ) = ( \mathbf { K } _ { X } ^ { \perp } \cap \mathbf { H } ^ { 2 } ( X , \mathbb { Z } ) ) / \mathbb { Z } \mathbf { K } _ { X }$ embeds naturally as a finite-index subgroup of $\operatorname { A u t } ( X )$ ; and this , /group acts by translation on the fibers of the elliptic fibration.
The action of any element $\alpha$ of ${ \mathfrak { L } } ( X )$ on the Picard group of $X$ is given by the explicit formula 10

$$
f _ { \alpha } ^ { * } ( D ) = D - m \left( D . { \mathrm { K } } _ { X } \right) \alpha + \left\{ m \left( D . \alpha \right) - \frac { m ^ { 2 } } { 2 } ( D . { \mathrm { K } } _ { X } ) \alpha ^ { 2 } \right\} { \mathrm { K } } _ { X } .
$$

In particular, $f _ { \alpha } ^ { * }$ acts unipotently on $\operatorname { P i c } ( X )$ , but $f _ { \alpha } ^ { * }$ is not of finite order (it is a true parabolic element).  
Thus $X$ α αhas no nonzero holomorphic vector field.

Proposition 3.19. Let $X$ be an unnodal Halphen surface of some index m carrying a smooth anticanonical curve, and let $\alpha$ be an element in the lattice ${ \mathfrak { L } } ( X )$ .
If $U$ is $a$ small neighborhood of $a$ point defining $X$ in ${ \mathcal { U } } _ { m } ,$ , let $\mathfrak { f } _ { \alpha }$ be a lift of $f _ { \alpha } ^ { 1 1 }$ on the family $( \mathfrak { H } _ { m } ) _ { | U }$ .
Then $\{ ( \mathfrak { H } _ { m } ) _ { | U } , \mathfrak { f } _ { \alpha } \}$ is a complete deformation of $( X , f _ { \alpha } )$ .

Proof.
Let $\{ X _ { t } , f _ { t } \} _ { t }$ be a local deformation of the pair $( X , f _ { \alpha } )$ .
Then for any $t$ , the automorphism $f _ { t }$ ,remains a parabolic isometry of $\mathrm { H } ^ { 2 } ( X _ { t } , \mathbb { Z } )$ , α.
Therefore, thanks to the main result of [21] (see [24]), $X _ { t }$ ,is a Halphen surface.
Now thanks to Proposition 3.1, we can write $X _ { t }$ as the blowup of nine points $p _ { i } ( t ) , 1 \leq i \leq 9$ varying holomorphically with $t$ .
For $t$ small enough, $X _ { t }$ is anticanonical so the points $p _ { i } ( t )$ , lie on a plane cubic curve $\mathcal { C } _ { t }$ .
Since $X _ { t }$ is Halphen, the point $\mathfrak { d } _ { t }$ is a torsion point in $\operatorname { P i c } ^ { 0 } ( \mathcal { C } _ { t } )$ .
We can see the map $t  \mathfrak { d } _ { t }$ as a local holomorphic section of the Jacobian variety of $X _ { t }$ whose values are torsion elements.
Since the order of ${ \mathfrak { d } } _ { 0 }$ is $m$ , it follows that all $\mathfrak { d } _ { t }$ have order $m$ .
Thus the points $p _ { i } ( t )$ define an element in ${ \mathcal { U } } _ { m }$ , which proves that $\{ X _ { t } \} _ { t }$ is obtained by pullback from ${ \mathfrak { H } } _ { m }$ and then $\{ X _ { t } , f _ { t } \} _ { t }$ is obtained by pullback from $\{ ( \mathfrak { H } _ { m } ) _ { | U } , \mathfrak { f } _ { \alpha } \}$ .


The proof of Proposition 3.19 relies heavily on Gizatullin’s result.
Let us explain how it is possible to obtain this result (at least for $m \geq 2$ ) using our method.

Proposition 3.20. Let X be an unnodal Halphen surface of index m carrying a smooth anticanonical curve.
Then there is a natural exact sequence

$$
0 \longrightarrow \mathbb { C } \longrightarrow { \mathrm { H } } ^ { 1 } ( X , { \mathrm { T X } } ) ^ { * } \longrightarrow { \mathrm { K } } _ { X } ^ { \perp } \longrightarrow 0
$$

of right ${ \mathfrak { L } } ( X )$ -modules, where ${ \mathrm { K } } _ { X } ^ { \perp }$ denotes the orthogonal of the canonical class in Pic $( X )$ , and ${ \mathfrak { L } } ( X )$ acts trivially on $\mathbb { C } .$ .
In particular the action of ${ \mathfrak { L } } ( X )$ on $\mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( X , \mathrm { T } X )$ is unipotent and for any $\alpha$ in ${ \mathfrak { L } } ( X ) _ { : }$ ,

$$
8 \leq \dim ( ( f _ { \alpha } ) _ { * } - \mathrm { i d } ) \leq 9
$$

where $( f _ { \alpha } ) _ { * }$ is the action of $f _ { \alpha }$ on $\mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( X , \mathrm { T } X )$ .

Remark 3.21. Proposition 3.19 is stronger because it gives dim ker (( fα)∗ − id) = ( 8 if m = 19 if m ≥ 2

Proof.
We adapt the proof of Theorem 3.11 in this situation.
To do so, we study the conormal exact sequence (3.4) of $C$ .

Assume that $\mathbf { m } \ge 2$ .
Since $\Nu _ { C / X } ^ { * }$ is a torsion point of order $m$ in $\mathrm { P i c } ^ { 0 } ( C ) , \mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( C , \mathrm { N } _ { C / X } ^ { * } ) = \{ 0 \}$ , so that by Riemann-Roch $\mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( C , \mathrm { N } _ { C / X } ^ { * } ) = \{ 0 \}$ .
Hence we get isomorphisms

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \mathrm { H } ^ { i } ( C , \Omega _ { X | C } ^ { 1 } ) \simeq \mathrm { H } ^ { i } ( C , \Omega _ { C } ^ { 1 } ) \qquad i \in \{ 0 , 1 \} , } \end{array}
$$

so that the kernel of the restriction map $\mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( X , \Omega _ { _ X } ^ { 1 } )  \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( C , \Omega _ { _ { X | C } } ^ { 1 } )$ identifies with ${ \mathrm { K } } _ { X } ^ { \perp }$ .
Since ${ \mathfrak { L } } ( X )$ acts trivially on $\mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( C , \Omega _ { C } ^ { 1 } )$ , the result follows using (3.4).

Assume that $\mathbf { m } = \mathbf { 1 }$ .
For any smooth fiber $C _ { t }$ of the pencil, $\mathrm { N } _ { C _ { t } / X }$ is canonically isomorphic to $O _ { C _ { t } } \otimes _ { \mathbb { C } } \mathbf { T } _ { t } \mathbb { P } ^ { 1 }$ /and the extension class of the normal exact sequence (which is dual to (3.4)) identifies with the Kodaira-Spencer map of the family $\{ C _ { s } \} _ { | s - t | < \epsilon }$ via the isomorphism

$$
\operatorname { E x t } _ { { \cal O } _ { C _ { t } } } ^ { 1 } ( C _ { t } , { \cal O } _ { C _ { t } } \otimes _ { \mathbb { C } } \Pi _ { t } \mathbb { P } ^ { 1 } , \Pi C _ { t } ) \simeq \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( C _ { t } , \Pi C _ { t } ) \otimes _ { \mathbb { C } } \Pi _ { t } ^ { * } \mathbb { P } ^ { 1 } .
$$

There are Halphen surfaces of index 1 such that the complex structure of the smooth fibers of the elliptic pencil remains constant (they are described in [21, Prop. B]) but they are not unnodal.
Thus, if $C$ is a generic fiber of the elliptic fibration, the Kodaira-Spencer map $\kappa \colon \mathrm { T } \mathbb { P } ^ { 1 } \to \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( C , \mathrm { T } C )$ is nonzero.
It follows that (3.4) is not holomorphically split over $O _ { C }$ κ, so that the maps

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( C , \mathrm { N } _ { C / X } ^ { * } )  \mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( C , \Omega _ { X | C } ^ { 1 } ) \qquad \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } \qquad \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( C , \Omega _ { X | C } ^ { 1 } )  \mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( C , \Omega _ { C } ^ { 1 } ) } \end{array}
$$

are isomorphisms.
We conclude using (3.4) again.

As a corollary, we get a new proof of Proposition 3.19 for $m \ge 2$ : indeed, the family $\{ ( \mathfrak { H } _ { m } ) _ { | U } , \mathfrak { f } _ { \alpha } \}$ is parameterized by a smooth base of dimension 17 which is stable under the action of $\mathrm { P G L } ( 3 ; \mathbb { C } )$ .
Thanks to Proposition 2.2, its Kodaira-Spencer map has rank 9 at any point of $U$ .
Then the conclusion follows from Theorem 2.7.

# 4. Kummer surfaces

By definition, a Kummer surface $X$ is a desingularization of a quotient ${ \mathcal { A } } / G$ where $\mathcal { A }$ is an abelian surface and $G$ is a finite group of automorphisms of $A$ /.
These subgroups have been classified (see [19]), and the geometry of the corresponding Kummer surfaces have been studied in [55].
Many situations can occur.
The most famous case is $G = \{ \pm \mathrm { i d } \}$ , and in this case $X$ is a K3 surface.
In this part, we will deal with two special pairs $( { \mathcal { A } } , G )$ such that $X$ is a rational surface.
There are many ,other cases apart these two ones where this happens (see [55, Thm 2.1]).

4.1. Rational Kummer surface associated with the hexagonal lattice.

4.1.1. Basic properties.
Let $\varepsilon$ be the elliptic curve obtained by taking the quotient of the complex line $\mathbb { C }$ by the lattice $\Lambda = \mathbb { Z } [ \mathbf { j } ]$ of Eisenstein integers, and let $\mathcal { A }$ be the abelian surface $\varepsilon \times \varepsilon$ , and let $\phi$ be the automorphism of order 3 defined by $\phi ( x , y ) = ( \mathbf { j } x , \mathbf { j } y )$ Since $\phi ^ { 2 } = \phi ^ { - 1 }$ , the automorphisms $\phi$ and $\phi ^ { 2 }$ φhave the same 9 fixed points which are

$$
{ \left\{ \begin{array} { l l l } { p _ { 1 } = ( 0 , 0 ) } & { p _ { 2 } = \left( 0 , { \frac { 2 } { 3 } } + { \frac { \mathbf { j } } { 3 } } \right) } & { p _ { 3 } = \left( 0 , { \frac { 1 } { 3 } } + { \frac { 2 \mathbf { j } } { 3 } } \right) } \\ { p _ { 4 } = \left( { \frac { 2 } { 3 } } + { \frac { \mathbf { j } } { 3 } } , 0 \right) } & { p _ { 5 } = \left( { \frac { 2 } { 3 } } + { \frac { \mathbf { j } } { 3 } } , { \frac { 2 } { 3 } } + { \frac { \mathbf { j } } { 3 } } \right) } & { p _ { 6 } = \left( { \frac { 2 } { 3 } } + { \frac { \mathbf { j } } { 3 } } , { \frac { 1 } { 3 } } + { \frac { 2 \mathbf { j } } { 3 } } \right) } \\ { p _ { 7 } = \left( { \frac { 1 } { 3 } } + { \frac { 2 \mathbf { j } } { 3 } } , 0 \right) } & { p _ { 8 } = \left( { \frac { 1 } { 3 } } + { \frac { 2 \mathbf { j } } { 3 } } , { \frac { 2 } { 3 } } + { \frac { \mathbf { j } } { 3 } } \right) } & { p _ { 9 } = \left( { \frac { 1 } { 3 } } + { \frac { 2 \mathbf { j } } { 3 } } , { \frac { 1 } { 3 } } + { \frac { 2 \mathbf { j } } { 3 } } \right) } \end{array} \right. }
$$

We denote this set by $S$ , it is a subgroup of the 3-torsion points in $\mathcal { A }$ .
Let $G$ be the group of order 3 generated by $\phi$ in $\operatorname { A u t } ( { \mathcal { A } } )$ .
Then ${ \mathcal { A } } / G$ is a singular surface, and the nine singularities corresponding φto the points of $S$ are of type $A _ { 3 }$ /.
Their blowup produces a smooth projective surface $X$ called a rational Kummer surface, and the nine exceptional divisors are of self-intersection $^ { - 3 }$ .

To avoid using singular surfaces, we use a slightly different construction yielding the same surface $X$ : first we blow up the set $S$ in $\mathcal { A }$ and denote by $\widetilde { \mathcal { A } }$ the resulting surface and by $\widetilde { E } _ { i }$ the exceptional divisors corresponding to the points $p _ { i }$ .
The group $G$ acts on $\mathcal { \bar { A } }$ , and the quotient ${ \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } } / G$ is $X$ .
For $1 \leq i \leq 9$ , let $E _ { i }$ be the image of $\widetilde { E } _ { i }$ in $X$ , it is a rational curve of self-intersection $^ { - 3 }$ .
We have the following diagram, where $\delta \colon { \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } }  { \mathcal { A } }$ is the blowup map and $\pi \colon { \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } } \to X$ is the projection

![](images/0950359f10d9156f7d3f97cc6e919e88e44c9f9b3213f90877aea3d13ee40e5f.jpg)

We can describe more precisely the map $\pi$ : $( \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } , \pi )$ is the cyclic covering of $X$ of order 3 branched along the rational curves $E _ { i }$ π.
In particular, for $1 \leq i \leq 9$ , we have $\pi ^ { * } \bar { E _ { i } } = 3 \widetilde { E _ { i } }$ .
Let us recall the following well-known fact (see [9, Ex.4 p. 103]):

Lemma 4.1. The surface $X$ is a basic rational surface that can be obtained by blowing 12 distinct points in $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ .

Proof.
Let us consider the four following curves $( C _ { i } ) _ { 1 \leq i \leq 4 }$ in $\mathcal { A }$

$$
\begin{array} { r } { C _ { 1 } = E \times \{ 0 \} , \quad C _ { 2 } = \{ 0 \} \times E , \quad C _ { 3 } = \Delta _ { \mathcal { A } } , \quad C _ { 4 } = \Gamma _ { - \phi } } \end{array}
$$

where $\Delta _ { \mathcal { A } }$ is the diagonal of $\mathcal { A }$ and $\Gamma _ { - \phi }$ is the graph of $- \phi$ .
Define 8 other curves $( C _ { i } ) _ { 5 \leq i \leq 1 2 }$ as follows:

$$
\left\{ { \begin{array} { c c c c } { C _ { 5 } = C _ { 1 } + p _ { 2 } } & { C _ { 6 } = C _ { 2 } + p _ { 4 } } & { C _ { 7 } = C _ { 3 } + p _ { 4 } } & { C _ { 8 } = C _ { 4 } + p _ { 4 } } \\ { C _ { 9 } = C _ { 1 } + p _ { 3 } } & { C _ { 1 0 } = C _ { 2 } + p _ { 7 } } & { C _ { 1 1 } = C _ { 3 } + p _ { 7 } } & { C _ { 1 2 } = C _ { 4 } + p _ { 7 } . } \end{array} } \right.
$$

![](images/f236ed3636728a991f99ed5e84c1064b974098dc4389034901b3f3c587867679.jpg)

The strict transforms of the 12 curves $C _ { i } ( 1 \leq i \leq 1 2 )$ in $\widetilde { \mathcal { A } }$ are $\phi$ -invariant elliptic curves of selfintersection $^ { - 3 }$ φ, since each of them pass through exactly three points of $S$ with multiplicity one).
Their images by $\pi$ give 12 smooth rational curves $( \mathcal { E } _ { i } ) _ { 1 \leq i \leq 1 2 }$ of self-intersection $^ { - 1 }$ .
Blowing down πthese 12 curves, we get a smooth surface $Y$ .
Since ${ \mathrm { H } } ^ { 1 } ( { \mathcal { A } } , \mathbb { Z } ) ^ { G } \simeq ( \Lambda ^ { * } \times \Lambda ^ { * } ) ^ { G } = \{ 0 \} ^ { 1 2 }$ where $\Lambda ^ { * }$ denotes the dual lattice of $\Lambda$ , ${ \sf b } _ { 1 } ( Y )$ , vanishes.
Now we compute the Euler characteristic of $Y$ :

$$
\langle ( Y ) = \chi ( X ) - 1 2 = \chi ( X \setminus \{ E _ { i } \} _ { i } ) + 6 = \frac { \chi ( \widetilde { A } \setminus \{ \widetilde { E } _ { i } \} _ { i } ) } { 3 } + 6 = \frac { \chi ( \widetilde { A } ) } { 3 } = \chi ( A ) + 3 = 3 = \chi ( \mathbb { P } ^ { 2 } )
$$

so that $b _ { 2 } ( Y ) = 1$ .
To conclude that the surface $Y$ is isomorphic to $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ and not to a fake projective plane, it suffices to prove that $Y$ is not a surface of general type (see [23, p. 487]).
Denoting by $\pmb { K }$ the Kodaira dimension, we have $\kappa ( Y ) = \kappa ( X ) \leq \kappa ( \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } ) = \kappa ( \mathcal { A } ) = 0$ .
This finishes the proof.


Remark 4.2. After blowing down the 12 exceptional curves, the exceptional divisors $E _ { i }$ are lines in $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ , since they are of self-intersection one.
Each point belongs to three lines and each line passes through four points.

The Picard group of $X$ can be described explicitly in the following way: since $X$ is rational, $\operatorname { P i c } ( X )$ is isomorphic to $\mathrm { H } ^ { 2 } ( X , \mathbb { Z } _ { X } )$ .
First we compute:

$$
\mathrm { H } ^ { 2 } ( \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } , \mathbb { Z } _ { \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } } ) \simeq \mathrm { H } ^ { 2 } ( \mathcal { R } , \mathbb { Z } _ { \mathcal { A } } ) \oplus \left( \bigoplus _ { i = 1 } ^ { 9 } \mathbb { Z } [ \widetilde { E } _ { i } ] \right) \simeq \wedge _ { \mathbb { Z } } ^ { 2 } \left( \Lambda ^ { * } \times \Lambda ^ { * } \right) \oplus \left( \bigoplus _ { i = 1 } ^ { 9 } \mathbb { Z } [ \widetilde { E } _ { i } ] \right) .
$$

Hence we get an isomorphism of ${ \mathrm { G L } } ( 2 ; \Lambda )$ -modules:

$$
\operatorname { P i c } ( X ) \simeq \mathrm { H } ^ { 2 } ( \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } , \mathbb { Z } _ { \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } } ) ^ { G } \simeq \left( \wedge _ { \mathbb { Z } } ^ { 2 } ( \Lambda ^ { * } \times \Lambda ^ { * } ) \right) ^ { G } \oplus \bigoplus _ { i = 1 } ^ { 9 } \mathbb { Z } [ \widetilde { E } _ { i } ] .
$$

It is easy to see that $\left( \wedge _ { \mathbb { Z } } ^ { 2 } ( \Lambda ^ { * } \times \Lambda ^ { * } ) \right) ^ { G }$ is a free $\mathbb { Z }$ -module of rank 4, a basis (over $\mathbb { Q }$ ) of this module being given by the curves $( C _ { i } ) _ { 1 \leq i \leq 4 }$ .
Thus $\operatorname { P i c } ( X )$ is a free $\mathbb { Z }$ -module of rank 13 (which is 1 plus the the number of points blown up, as expected).
We end this section by a description of the canonical class of $X$ .

Lemma 4.3. We have $\begin{array} { r } { | - \mathbf { K } _ { X } | = | - 2 \mathbf { K } _ { X } | = \emptyset a n d | - 3 \mathbf { K } _ { X } | = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { 9 } E _ { i } . } \end{array}$

Proof.
Since $\pi$ is a cyclic covering, the generalized Riemann-Hurwitz formula for branched cyclic covers gives $\pi ^ { * } \textstyle \mathbf { K } _ { X } = \textstyle \mathbf { K } _ { \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } } ( - 2 \textstyle \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { 9 } \widetilde { E } _ { i } )$ .
On the other hand, $\begin{array} { r } { \mathrm { K } _ { \mathcal { \widetilde { A } } } = \delta ^ { * } \mathrm { K } _ { \mathcal { A } } + \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { 9 } \widetilde { E } _ { i } } \end{array}$ so that $\pi ^ { * } \mathrm { K } _ { X } =$ $- \textstyle \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { 9 } { \widetilde { E } } _ { i }$ π.
It follows that $\begin{array} { r } { - 3 \mathsf { K } _ { X } \sim \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { 9 } E _ { i } } \end{array}$ .
Now if $D$ ebelongs to $\lvert - 3 \mathtt { K } _ { X } \rvert$ , we have $D . E _ { i } = - 3$ π, so that $D$ must contain $E _ { i }$ as a component.
Thus $\begin{array} { r } { D = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { 9 } E _ { i } + D ^ { \prime } } \end{array}$ where $D ^ { \prime }$ .is effective.
But $D ^ { \prime } \sim 0$ so that $D ^ { \prime } = 0$ and $\begin{array} { r } { \vert - 3 \mathbf { K } _ { X } \vert = \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { 9 } E _ { i } } \end{array}$ .


4.1.2. Linear automorphisms of the Kummer surface.
The group ${ \mathrm { G L } } ( 2 ; \Lambda )$ acts linearly on $\mathbb { C } ^ { 2 }$ and preserves the lattice $\Lambda \times \Lambda$ Therefore any element $M$ of ${ \mathrm { G L } } ( 2 ; \Lambda )$ induces an automorphism $f _ { M }$ on $\mathcal { A }$ .that commutes with the automorphism $\phi$ of $A$ , hence leaves the set $S$ globally invariant.
Each $f _ { M }$ lifts to an automorphism $\tilde { f } _ { M }$ φof the blown up abelian surface $\widetilde { \mathcal { A } }$ that still commutes to the action of the group $G$ generated by $\phi$ .
Thus $\tilde { f } _ { M }$ descends to an automorphism $\varphi _ { M }$ of $X$ .
The map $M \to \varphi _ { M }$ embeds ${ \mathrm { G L } } ( 2 ; \Lambda ) / G$ φas a subgroup of Aut $( X )$ .

Let $H$ be the group of matrices in $\operatorname { S L } ( 2 ; \Lambda )$ that are congruent to the identity matrix modulo the ideal $( 1 - \mathbf { j } ) \mathbb { Z } [ \mathbf { j } ]$ .

Lemma 4.4. The natural morphism $H \to \operatorname { \mathrm { { G L } } } ( 2 ; \Lambda ) / G$ is injective and its image is exactly the stabilizer of $S$ .

Proof.
The injectivity is obvious since $G \cap { \mathrm { S L } } ( 2 ; \Lambda ) = { \mathrm { i d } }$ .
For the surjectivity, note that the automorphism $f _ { M }$ fixes the nine points $p _ { i }$ if an only if 3 divides $( 2 + \mathbf { j } ) ( a - 1 ) , ( 2 + \mathbf { j } ) b , ( 2 + \mathbf { j } ) c$ and $\left( 2 + \mathbf { j } \right) \left( d - 1 \right)$ where $M = { \Bigl ( } { \begin{array} { l l } { a } & { b } \\ { c } & { d } \end{array} } { \Bigr ) }$ .
Since $3 = ( 2 + \mathbf { j } ) ( 1 - \mathbf { j } ) ,$ $M$ belongs to the stabilizer of $S$ if and only if its reduction modulo $( 1 - \mathbf { j } ) \mathbb { Z } [ \mathbf { j } ]$ is the identity matrix.
Among the units of $\mathbb { Z } [ { \bf j } ]$ , only $1 , \mathbf { j }$ and $\mathbf { j } ^ { 2 }$ are congruent to 1 modulo $1 - \mathbf { j }$ .
Thus det $M \in \{ 1 , \mathbf { j } , \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } \}$ and we are done.


Let $M$ be an element of ${ \mathrm { G L } } ( 2 ; \Lambda )$ with eigenvalues $\alpha$ and $\beta$ , and $V = ( \Lambda ^ { * } \times \Lambda ^ { * } ) \otimes _ { \mathbb { Z } } \mathbb { C }$ .
Then the complex eigenvalues of the endomorphism $\wedge ^ { 2 } M$ α βof the real vector space $\wedge _ { \mathbb { R } } ^ { 2 } V$ are $\vert \alpha \vert ^ { 2 } , \vert \beta \vert ^ { 2 } , \alpha \overline { { { \beta } } } , \overline { { { \alpha } } } \beta$ , $\alpha \beta$ and $\overline { { \alpha } } \overline { { \beta } }$ .
Note that for dimension reasons we have a $G$ -equivariant exact sequence

$$
0 \longrightarrow \left( \wedge _ { \mathbb { R } } ^ { 2 } V \right) ^ { G } \longrightarrow \wedge _ { \mathbb { R } } ^ { 2 } V \longrightarrow \wedge _ { \mathbb { C } } ^ { 2 } V \longrightarrow 0
$$

since $\phi$ acts by multiplication by $\mathbf { j } ^ { 2 }$ on $\wedge _ { \mathbb { C } } ^ { 2 } V$ .
Besides $f _ { M }$ acts by de $\iota ( M ) = \alpha \beta$ on $\wedge _ { \mathbb { C } } ^ { 2 } V$ , so the eigenvalues of the corresponding $\mathbb { R }$ -linear endormorphism are $\alpha \beta$ and $\overline { { \alpha } } \overline { { \beta } }$ .
Thus the eigenvalues of $\wedge ^ { 2 } M$ on the subspace ${ \bar { ( \land _ { \mathbb { R } } ^ { 2 } \ V ) } } ^ { G }$ are exactly $| \alpha | ^ { 2 } , | \beta | ^ { 2 } , \alpha \overline { { \beta } }$ and $\overline { { \alpha } } \beta$ αβ αβ.
We conclude that for any element $M$ in ${ \mathrm { G L } } ( 2 ; \Lambda )$ with spectral radius $r _ { M }$ α β αβ, the spectral radius of $M$ βacting on ${ \mathrm { N S } } _ { \mathbb { Q } } ( X )$ is $r _ { M } ^ { 2 }$ .
This means that the first dynamical degree of $\tilde { f } _ { M }$ is given by the formula $\lambda _ { 1 } ( \tilde { f } _ { M } ) = r _ { M } ^ { 2 }$ .
More precisely, the characteristic polynomial of $\tilde { f } _ { M } ^ { * }$ acting on ${ \mathrm { N S } } _ { \mathbb { Q } } ( X )$ is

$$
( x - 1 ) ^ { 9 } ( x - | \alpha | ^ { 2 } ) ( x - | \beta | ^ { 2 } ) ( x - \overline { { \alpha } } \beta ) ( x - \alpha \overline { { \beta } } ) .
$$

4.1.3. Explicit realisation in the Cremona group.
According to Lemma 4.1, the Kummer surface $X$ is obtained by blowing up $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ along 12 points, and an explicit morphism $p \colon X \to \mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ is obtained by blowing down the $^ { - 1 }$ curves $( \mathcal { E } _ { i } ) _ { 1 \leq i \leq 1 2 }$ .
We can therefore define for any matrix $M$ in $\operatorname { G L } ( 2 ; \mathbb { Z } [ { \mathbf { j } } ] )$ a Cremona transformation $\psi _ { M } \colon { \mathbb { P } } ^ { 2 } \to { \mathbb { P } } ^ { 2 }$ by the formula

$$
\psi _ { M } = p ^ { - 1 } \circ \varphi _ { M } \circ p .
$$

It is an interesting question to find explicit formulas for the map $\psi _ { M }$ .
The first step to understand the maps $\psi _ { M }$ is to describe explicitly the configuration of lines $( p ( E _ { i } ) ) _ { 1 \leq i \leq 9 }$ in $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ since the intersection ψpoints of these lines give the indeterminacy locus of $p ^ { - 1 }$ .
Let us put

$$
\left\{ \begin{array} { l l } { q _ { i } = p ( \mathcal { E } _ { i } ) } & { \mathrm { f o r } \quad 1 \leq i \leq 1 2 } \\ { \Delta _ { j } = p ( E _ { j } ) } & { \mathrm { f o r } \quad 1 \leq j \leq 9 . } \end{array} \right.
$$

The two configurations $\{ C _ { i } , p _ { j } \}$ and $\{ \Delta _ { j } , q _ { i } \}$ are projectively dual.

![](images/282ec401b7bd013d5fe95f40c3d5767011758274fab83f9896e5a5c974941bcc.jpg)

We have the following result (which is almost [37, Proposition 1] with a small additional ingredient).

Proposition 4.5. There exists linear coordinates $x ,$ , y, z on $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ such that

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \left\{ \begin{array} { l l l l l } { \Delta _ { 1 } = \{ y = z \} } & { \Delta _ { 2 } = \{ y = { \bf j } z \} } & { \Delta _ { 3 } = \{ y = { \bf j } ^ { 2 } z \} } & { \Delta _ { 4 } = \{ x = z \} } & { \Delta _ { 5 } = \{ x = { \bf j } ^ { 2 } z \} } \\ { \Delta _ { 6 } = \{ x = { \bf j } z \} } & { \Delta _ { 7 } = \{ y = x \} } & { \Delta _ { 8 } = \{ y = { \bf j } ^ { 2 } x \} } & { \Delta _ { 9 } = \{ y = { \bf j } x \} } \end{array} \right. } \end{array}
$$

and

$$
\left\{ \begin{array} { l l l l } { q _ { 1 } = ( 1 : 1 : 1 ) } & { q _ { 2 } = ( 1 : 0 : 0 ) } & { q _ { 3 } = ( { \bf j } ^ { 2 } : 1 : 1 ) } & { q _ { 4 } = ( { \bf j } : 1 : 1 ) } \\ { q _ { 5 } = ( { \bf j } ^ { 2 } : { \bf j } : 1 ) } & { q _ { 6 } = ( 0 : 1 : 0 ) } & { q _ { 7 } = ( 1 : { \bf j } ^ { 2 } : 1 ) } & { q _ { 8 } = ( 1 : { \bf j } : 1 ) } \\ { q _ { 9 } = ( { \bf j } : { \bf j } ^ { 2 } : 1 ) } & { q _ { 1 0 } = ( 0 : 0 : 1 ) } & { q _ { 1 1 } = ( { \bf j } : { \bf j } : 1 ) } & { q _ { 1 2 } = ( { \bf j } ^ { 2 } : { \bf j } ^ { 2 } : 1 ) } \end{array} \right.
$$

Proof.
Since no line passes through three points in the set $\{ q _ { 1 } , q _ { 2 } , q _ { 6 } , q _ { 1 0 } \}$ , there exist unique linear coordinates on $\mathbb { P } ^ { 2 }$ such that $q _ { 1 } = ( 1 : 1 : 1 )$ , $q _ { 2 } = ( 1 : 0 : 0 )$ , $q _ { 6 } = ( 0 : 1 : 0 )$ and $q _ { 1 0 } = ( 0 : 0 : 1 )$ .

Then $\Delta _ { 1 } = \{ y = z \}$ , ${ \Delta _ { 4 } } = \{ x = z \}$ and ${ \Delta } _ { 7 } = \{ x = y \}$ .
Now the line $\{ z = 0 \}$ (resp.
$\{ x = 0 \}$ ) cannot be equal to $\Delta _ { 5 }$ or $\Delta _ { 6 }$ since it passes through $q _ { 2 }$ (resp.
$q _ { 1 0 } \mathrm { , }$ ).
Hence there exist nonzero complex numbers $\alpha$ and $\beta$ such that $\Delta _ { 5 } = \{ x = \alpha z \}$ and $\Delta _ { 6 } = \{ x = \beta z \}$ .
Then $\Delta _ { 3 } = \{ y = \alpha z \}$ and $\Delta _ { 2 } = \{ y = \beta z \}$ .
Hence α βin the affine chart $z = 1$ , $q _ { 3 } = ( \alpha , 1 )$ , $q _ { 4 } = ( \beta , 1 )$ β, $q _ { 7 } = ( 1 , \alpha )$ , $q _ { 1 2 } = ( \alpha , \alpha )$ , $q _ { 9 } = ( \beta , \alpha )$ , $q _ { 8 } = ( 1 , \beta )$ , $q _ { 5 } = ( \alpha , \beta )$ and $q _ { 1 1 } = ( \beta , \beta )$ α,.
Using that $\{ q _ { 1 0 } , q _ { 3 } , q _ { 9 } , q _ { 8 } \}$ , α α, α are aligned, we get $\alpha ^ { 2 } = \beta$ β, αand $\alpha = \beta ^ { 2 }$ , βso that $\alpha \in \{ \mathbf { j } , \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } \}$ β, β , , , α β α β.
This gives two distinct configurations, which are not projectively isomorphic, although α ,they are complex conjugate 13. Using the affine coordinate $x$ as a coordinate on $\Delta _ { 1 }$ , we see that the cross-ratio $[ q _ { 1 } , q _ { 2 } , q _ { 3 } , q _ { 4 } ]$ is

$$
[ q _ { 1 } , q _ { 2 } , q _ { 3 } , q _ { 4 } ] = [ 1 , \infty , \alpha , \alpha ^ { 2 } ] = \frac { 1 - \alpha } { 1 - \alpha ^ { 2 } } = \frac { 1 } { 1 + \alpha } = - \alpha .
$$

But this cross ratio is easy to compute, since the points $q _ { i } , 1 \leq i \leq 4$ can be identified with the intersections of the strict transforms of the curves $C _ { i } , 1 \leq i \leq 4$ with the exceptional divisor $\widetilde { E } _ { 1 }$ via the isomorphisms $\widetilde { E } _ { 1 } \simeq E _ { 1 } \simeq \Delta _ { 1 }$ .
Hence we get

$$
[ q _ { 1 } , q _ { 2 } , q _ { 3 } , q _ { 4 } ] = [ 0 , \infty , 1 , - \mathbf { j } ] = - \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 }
$$

so that $\alpha = \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 }$ and we are done.

The next step to understand the birational maps $\psi _ { M }$ is to compute their degrees.
Although this result ψis not strictly necessary for us, we include it because it can be useful for explicit computations.

Proposition 4.6. For any matrix $M = \left( \begin{array} { c c } { { a } } & { { b } } \\ { { c } } & { { d } } \end{array} \right) i n { \bf G } { \bf L } ( 2 ; \mathbb { Z } [ { \bf j } ] ) ,$

$$
{ \bf \xi } _ { M } = \left| a + d \right| ^ { 2 } + \left| c - \mathbf { j } b \right| ^ { 2 } + \left( 1 + \frac { \sqrt { 3 } } { 2 } \right) \left| \mathbf { i } a - \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } b - \mathbf { j } c - \mathbf { i } d \right| ^ { 2 } + \left( 1 - \frac { \sqrt { 3 } } { 2 } \right) \left| \mathbf { i } a + \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } b + \mathbf { j } c - \mathbf { i } d \right| ^ { 2 } - \frac { \sqrt { 3 } } { 2 } = 0 .
$$

Proof.
Let us recall the three following commutative diagrams:

$$
\begin{array} { r l r l r l } & { \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } \xrightarrow { \tilde { f } _ { M } } \sim \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } } & { \qquad } & { \widetilde { \mathcal { H } } \xrightarrow { \tilde { f } _ { M } } \widetilde { \mathcal { H } } } & { \qquad } & { X \xrightarrow { \varphi _ { M } } \setminus X } \\ & { \delta \Bigg | } & { \qquad \Bigg \downarrow \delta } & { \qquad } & { \left. \begin{array} { l } { \pi } \\ { \mathcal { H } } \\ { X \xrightarrow { \varphi _ { M } } \sim X } \end{array} \right| \pi } & { \qquad } & { \psi \Bigg \downarrow } & { \qquad \pmb { \operatorname { N } } } \\ & { \widetilde { \mathcal { P } } ^ { 2 } - \overset { \psi _ { M } } { \sim } \mathbb { P } ^ { 2 } } & { \qquad } & { X \xrightarrow { \varphi _ { M } } \setminus X } \end{array}
$$

We have $\begin{array} { r } { p ^ { * } \Delta _ { 1 } = E _ { 1 } + \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { 4 } \mathcal { E } _ { i } ^ { \it } } \end{array}$ Hence, if $\zeta$ and $\epsilon$ denote the divisors $\textstyle \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { 4 } C _ { i }$ and $\textstyle \sum _ { i = 1 } ^ { 9 } { \widetilde { E } } _ { i }$ on $\mathcal { A }$ and $\widetilde { \mathcal { A } }$ respectively,

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } { ( p \circ \pi ) ^ { \ast } \Delta _ { 1 } = 3 \widetilde { E } _ { 1 } + ( \delta ^ { \ast } C _ { 1 } - \widetilde { E } _ { 1 } - \widetilde { E } _ { 4 } - \widetilde { E } _ { 7 } ) + ( \delta ^ { \ast } C _ { 2 } - \widetilde { E } _ { 1 } - \widetilde { E } _ { 2 } - \widetilde { E } _ { 3 } ) } & { } & \\ { + ( \delta ^ { \ast } C _ { 3 } - \widetilde { E } _ { 1 } - \widetilde { E } _ { 5 } - \widetilde { E } _ { 9 } ) + ( \delta ^ { \ast } C _ { 4 } - \widetilde { E } _ { 1 } - \widetilde { E } _ { 4 } - \widetilde { E } _ { 8 } ) } & { } & \\ { = \delta ^ { \ast } \zeta \ - \epsilon } & { } & \end{array}
$$

so that

$$
\psi _ { M } = p ^ { * } \Delta _ { 1 } . ( p \circ \varphi _ { M } ) ^ { * } \Delta _ { 1 } = \frac { ( p \circ \varphi _ { M } \circ \pi ) ^ { * } \Delta _ { 1 } . ( p \circ \pi ) ^ { * } \Delta _ { 1 } } { 3 } = \frac { f _ { M } ^ { * } \zeta . \zeta + \widetilde { f } _ { M } ^ { * } \epsilon . \epsilon } { 3 } = \frac { f _ { M } ^ { * } \zeta . \zeta } { 3 } - 3
$$

A routine computation yields the value of the cohomology classes of the curves $C _ { i }$ in $\mathrm { H } ^ { 2 } ( \mathcal { R } , \mathbb { C } )$ :

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { { \displaystyle { [ C _ { 1 } ] = \frac { \mathbf { i } \sqrt { 3 } } { 3 } d z \wedge d \bar { z } } } } } \\ { { { } } } \\ { { { \displaystyle { [ C _ { 2 } ] = \frac { \mathbf { i } \sqrt { 3 } } { 3 } d w \wedge d \bar { w } } } } } \\ { { { } } } \\ { { { \displaystyle { [ C _ { 3 } ] = \frac { \mathbf { i } \sqrt { 3 } } { 3 } ( d \bar { z } \wedge d w - d z \wedge d \bar { w } + d z \wedge d \bar { z } + d w \wedge d \bar { w } ) } } } } \\ { { { } } } \\ { { { \displaystyle { [ C _ { 4 } ] = \frac { \mathbf { i } \sqrt { 3 } } { 3 } \left( \mathbf { j } d z \wedge d \bar { w } - \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } d \bar { z } \wedge d w + d z \wedge d \bar { z } + d w \wedge d \bar { w } \right) } . } } } \end{array}
$$

Thus, if $\mu = ( \mathbf { j } - 1 )$ ,

$$
[ \zeta ] = { \frac { \mathbf { i } { \sqrt { 3 } } } { 3 } } \left( 3 d z \wedge d { \bar { z } } + 3 d w \wedge d { \bar { w } } + \mu d z \wedge d { \bar { w } } - { \bar { \mu } } d { \bar { z } } \wedge d w \right)
$$

so that

$$
\deg \psi _ { M } = 3 ( | a | ^ { 2 } + | b | ^ { 2 } + | c | ^ { 2 } + | d | ^ { 2 } ) + 2 \Re \left\{ ( \mathbf { j } - 1 ) ( a \bar { c } + b \bar { d } - \bar { a } b - \bar { c } d ) + \mathbf { j } b \bar { c } - a \bar { d } \right\} - 3 .
$$

The result follows by a direct calculation.

# Remark 4.7.

(i) The degree of $\psi _ { M }$ depends only on the class of $M$ in ${ \mathrm { G L } } ( 2 ; \Lambda ) / G$ , and this can be verified ψdirectly on the explicit expression of $\deg \psi _ { M }$ /given by Proposition 4.6.

(ii) We have $\deg \psi _ { M } = Q ( a , b , c , d ) - 3$ where $Q$ is a positive-definite hermitian form.
Hence for ψany positive integer $N$ , , ,, the set

$$
\left\{ M \in \mathrm { G L } ( 2 ; \mathbb { Z } [ { \bf j } ] ) \mathrm { ~ s . t . ~ } \deg \Psi _ { M } \leq N \right\}
$$

is finite.

Theorem 4.8. The matrices $M _ { 1 } = { \left( \begin{array} { l l } { 0 } & { 1 } \\ { - 1 } & { 1 } \end{array} \right) } , M _ { 2 } = { \left( \begin{array} { l l } { 0 } & { 1 } \\ { - \mathbf { j } } & { 0 } \end{array} \right) }$ and $M _ { 3 } = { \binom { 0 } { 1 } } \ 1$ generate $\mathbf { G L } ( 2 ; \mathbb { Z } [ \mathbf { j } ] )$ as a semigroup.
Besides, the corresponding Cremona transformations have the following explicit description:

$$
\left\{ \begin{array} { l l } { \psi _ { M _ { 1 } } : ( x : y : z ) \to ( x + y + z : \mathbf { j } x + y + \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } z : \mathbf { j } x + \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } y + z ) } \\ { \psi _ { M _ { 2 } } : ( x : y : z ) \to ( x + y + z : x + \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } y + \mathbf { j } z : x + \mathbf { j } y + \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } z ) } \\ { \psi _ { M _ { 3 } } : ( x : y : z ) \to ( a ( x : y : z ) , b ( x : y : z ) , c ( x : y : z ) ) } \end{array} \right.
$$

where

$$
\left\{ \begin{array} { l l } { a ( x : y : z ) = x ^ { 2 } + y ^ { 2 } + z ^ { 2 } - \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } ( x y + x z + y z ) } \\ { b ( x : y : z ) = x ^ { 2 } + \mathbf { j } y ^ { 2 } + \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } z ^ { 2 } - \mathbf { j } x y - x z - \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } y z } \\ { c ( x : y : z ) = x ^ { 2 } + \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } y ^ { 2 } + \mathbf { j } z ^ { 2 } - x y - \mathbf { j } x z - \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } y z } \end{array} \right.
$$

Proof.
The ring $\mathbb { Z } [ { \bf j } ]$ being euclidean, any matrix in $\operatorname { G L } ( 2 ; \mathbb { Z } [ { \mathbf { j } } ] )$ can be put in diagonal form after performing elementary row and column operations, corresponding to multiplications on the right and on the left by matrices of the type ${ \binom { 1 } { 0 } } \quad { \overset { \circ } { 1 } } { \overset { \circ } { \partial } } { \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } } { \overset { \circ } { \underset { \beta } { \beta } } } 1 { \overset { \circ } { \partial } } .$ First note that the diagonal matrices can be expressed using $M _ { 1 } , M _ { 2 }$ and $M _ { 3 }$ : we have

$$
\left( 1 \begin{array} { c c } { { 1 } } & { { 0 } } \\ { { 0 } } & { { - { \bf j } } } \end{array} \right) = M _ { 2 } M _ { 3 } \qquad \mathrm { a n d } \qquad M _ { 3 } M _ { 2 } M _ { 3 } ^ { 2 } = \left( \begin{array} { c c } { { { \bf j } } } & { { 0 } } \\ { { 0 } } & { { 1 } } \end{array} \right)
$$

and these two matrices generate all invertible diagonal matrices, since $\mathbb { Z } [ { \mathbf i } ] ^ { \times }$ is the cyclic subgroup of $\mathbb { C } ^ { \times }$ generated by $- \mathbf { j }$ .
We now deal with the transvection matrices:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } { \left( \begin{array} { c c } { 1 } & { 1 } \\ { 0 } & { 1 } \end{array} \right) = \left( \begin{array} { c c } { - 1 } & { 0 } \\ { 0 } & { - 1 } \end{array} \right) \times M _ { 3 } M _ { 1 } ( M _ { 2 } M _ { 3 } ) ^ { 2 } } & { { } } \\ { \left( \begin{array} { c c } { 1 } & { 0 } \\ { 1 } & { 1 } \end{array} \right) = \left( \begin{array} { c c } { - 1 } & { 0 } \\ { 0 } & { - 1 } \end{array} \right) \times M _ { 1 } ( M _ { 2 } M _ { 3 } ) ^ { 3 } M _ { 3 } } & { { } } \\ { \left( \begin{array} { c c } { 1 } & { \mathbf { j } } \\ { 0 } & { 1 } \end{array} \right) = \left( \begin{array} { c c } { \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } } & { 0 } \\ { 0 } & { \mathbf { j } } \end{array} \right) \times \left( \begin{array} { c c } { 1 } & { 1 } \\ { 0 } & { 1 } \end{array} \right) \times \left( \begin{array} { c c } { \mathbf { j } } & { 0 } \\ { 0 } & { \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } } \end{array} \right) } & { { } } \\ { \left( \begin{array} { c c } { 1 } & { 0 } \\ { \mathbf { j } } & { 1 } \end{array} \right) = \left( \begin{array} { c c } { \mathbf { j } } & { \mathbf { 0 } } \\ { \mathbf { j } } & { \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } } \end{array} \right) \times \left( \begin{array} { c c } { 1 } & { 0 } \\ { 1 } & { 1 } \end{array} \right) \times \left( \begin{array} { c c } { \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } } & { 0 } \\ { 0 } & { \mathbf { j } } \end{array} \right) } & { { } } \end{array}
$$

Hence $M _ { 1 } , M _ { 2 }$ and $M _ { 3 }$ generate $\operatorname { G L } ( 2 ; \mathbb { Z } [ { \mathbf { j } } ] )$ as a group.
Since these matrices are of finite order, they also generate $\operatorname { G L } ( 2 ; \mathbb { Z } [ { \mathbf { j } } ] )$ as a semigroup.

It remains to compute the explicit expressions of the $\psi _ { M _ { i } } , 1 \le i \le 3$ .
First it is easy to see that $\psi _ { M _ { 1 } }$ and $\psi _ { M _ { 2 } }$ ψ ,have no indeterminacy point, because the maps $\varphi _ { M _ { 1 } }$ and $\varphi _ { M _ { 2 } }$ preserve globally the ψ ψtwelve exceptional curves $\mathcal { E } _ { j } ^ { \mathrm { { ~ \scriptsize ~ \it ~ } } }$ $1 \leq j \leq 1 2$ .
Hence $\psi _ { M _ { 1 } }$ and $\psi _ { M _ { 2 } }$ ϕ ϕare linear (this could also be checked , ψ ψusing Proposition 4.6), and therefore entirely determined by its action on the points $q _ { k }$ .
In order to compute $\psi _ { M _ { 3 } }$ , we first note that it is a quadratic involution.
Indeed, $\varphi _ { M _ { 3 } }$ leaves globally invariant the set $\{ \mathcal { E } _ { 1 } , \mathcal { E } _ { 5 } , \mathcal { E } _ { 9 } , \mathcal { E } _ { 2 } , \mathcal { E } _ { 6 } , \mathcal { E } _ { 1 0 } , \mathcal { E } _ { 3 } , \mathcal { E } _ { 7 } , \mathcal { E } _ { 1 1 } \}$ ϕ, so that the indeterminacy locus of $\psi _ { M _ { 3 } }$ consists of simple , , ,points in the list $\{ q _ { 4 } , q _ { 8 } , q _ { 1 2 } \}$ , ,.
Since $\psi _ { M _ { 3 } }$ is not linear (otherwise all $\psi _ { M }$ ψwould also be linear), it , , ψmust be a quadratic involution whose indeterminacy locus is the set $\{ q _ { 4 } , q _ { 8 } , q _ { 1 2 } \}$ .
It is completely determined by its action on the remaining points points $q _ { k }$ for $k \notin \{ 4 , 8 , 1 2 \}$ .


4.2. Action of the automorphism group on infinitesimal deformations.
In this section, we will present two different approaches to prove the following theorem:

Theorem 4.9. Let $X$ be the rational Kummer surface associated with the lattice Z[j] of Eisenstein integers.
For any element $M$ in the group $\operatorname { G L } ( 2 ; \mathbb { Z } [ { \mathbf { j } } ] )$ , let $\sigma _ { M }$ be the permutation of $S$ given by the action of $f _ { M }$ , and let $\mathcal { P } _ { M }$ σbe the set of the 9 eigenvalues of the permutation matrix associated with $\sigma _ { M }$ .
Then the characteristic polynomial $Q _ { M }$ of the endomorphism $( \varphi _ { M } ^ { - 1 } ) _ { * }$ of $\mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( X , \mathrm { T } X )$ is given by σthe formula

$$
Q _ { M } ( x ) = \prod _ { \lambda \in \mathcal { P } _ { M } \backslash \{ 1 \} } \left( x - \frac { \lambda } { \alpha \beta ^ { 2 } } \right) \left( x - \frac { \lambda } { \alpha ^ { 2 } \beta } \right) .
$$

# Remarks 4.10.

(i) We have $\# \mathcal { P } _ { M } = 9$ , so that deg $Q _ { M } = 1 6 = 2 \times 1 2 - 8$ .  
(ii) If $M$ is in the group $G$ generated by $\phi$ , then $Q _ { M } ( x ) = ( x - 1 ) ^ { 1 6 }$ as expected.
Indeed, if $M = \left( \begin{array} { c c } { { { \bf j } } } & { { 0 } } \\ { { 0 } } & { { { \bf j } } } \end{array} \right)$ then $\alpha = \beta = \mathbf { j }$ and $\mathcal { P } _ { M } = \{ 1 , 1 , 1 , 1 , 1 , 1 \}$ .

# Corollary 4.11.

(i) If M is a matrix in $H$ , then $Q _ { M } ( x ) = ( x - \alpha ) ^ { 8 } ( x - \beta ) ^ { 8 }$ .  
(ii) If M is not of finite order in $\operatorname { G L } ( 2 ; \mathbb { Z } [ { \bf j } ] ) / \langle { \bf j } { \mathrm { i d } } \rangle ,$ , then $\varphi _ { M }$ is rigid.

The first approach of the proof of Theorem 4.9 will use the Atiyah-Bott fixed point theorem to compute the trace of the action of $\varphi _ { M }$ on $\mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( X , \mathrm { T } X )$ for $M$ in $H$ .
Knowing this for all iterates of $M$ , we obtain all eigenvalues of $\tilde { f } _ { M }$ ϕ ,.
We will limit ourselves to the case where $M$ is in $H$ (that is to the statement $( i )$ of Corollary 4.11), but all other cases can be dealt with using the same method.
Since these calculations don’t bring anything new conceptually (and since the second proof is valid for any $M$ in ${ \mathrm { G L } } ( 2 ; \Lambda ) )$ we omit them.
The other approach is to the machinery of sheaves: the tangent bundles TA, $\operatorname { T } \widetilde { \mathcal { A } }$ and TX are related by some exact sequences, allowing to compare their respective cohomology groups.

4.2.1. First proof by the Atiyah-Bott formula.
Let us divide the set of fixed points of $\varphi _ { M }$ into two parts: the first part $\Theta _ { 1 }$ consists of fixed points outside the exceptional divisors $E _ { i }$ ϕ; and the second part $\Theta _ { 2 }$ consists of the remaining fixed points.

Proposition 4.12. Let $M$ be a matrix in $H$ such that $M ^ { 3 } \ne$ id.
Then the automorphism $\varphi _ { M }$ has $| \mathrm { T r } ( M ) | ^ { 2 } + 1 1$ fixed points on the Kummer surface $X$ .

Proof.
The first step consists in proving that the fixed points of $\varphi _ { M }$ are non-degenerate, i.e. that 1 is never an eigenvalue of the differential of $\varphi _ { M }$ ϕat a fixed point.
We deal with fixed points in $\Theta _ { 1 }$ and $\Theta _ { 2 }$ separately.

The points of $\Theta _ { 1 }$ correspond to points $p$ in $A \setminus S$ such that $f _ { M } ( \boldsymbol { p } )$ lies in the orbit $G \cdot p$ , modulo the action of $G$ .
For any such point, the differential of $\varphi _ { M }$ identifies with $M , \mathbf { j } M$ or ${ \bf j } ^ { 2 } M$ , so that 1 is never an eigenvalue.

The set $\Theta _ { 2 }$ can be described as follows: the automorphism $\tilde { f } _ { M }$ acts by the projective transformation $\mathbb { P } ( M )$ on the curve $E _ { i }$ via the identification

$$
E _ { i } \simeq { \widetilde { E } } _ { i } \simeq \mathbb { P } ( \mathrm { T } _ { p _ { i } } V ) \simeq \mathbb { P } ( V ) .
$$

Therefore we get two fixed points $q _ { \alpha }$ and $q _ { \beta }$ on each $E _ { i }$ corresponding to the eigenspaces of $M$ .
This gives a concrete description of $\Theta _ { 2 }$ α β, which consists of 18 points (two distinct points on each $E _ { i }$ ).
To compute the differential of $\tilde { f } _ { M }$ at such a fixed point, we can assume without loss of generality that this point lies in $E _ { 1 }$ .
Let $( e , f )$ be an eigenvector of $M$ for the eigenvalue $\alpha$ and assume for simplicity that $e \neq 0$ .
If $( x , y )$ , are the standard coordinates on $V$ α, we define coordinates $( u , \nu )$ in $\widetilde { \mathcal { A } }$ near $\widetilde { E } _ { 1 }$ by putting $x = u$ ,and $y = u \nu$ .
Then if we set $Z = u ^ { 3 }$ and $T = \nu$ , $( Z , T )$ , are holomorphic coordinates on

$X$ near $q _ { \alpha }$ .
In these coordinates, $q _ { \alpha } = \left( 0 , { \frac { f } { e } } \right)$ and $\tilde { f } _ { M }$ is given by

$$
( Z , T ) \mapsto \left( Z ( a + b T ) ^ { 3 } , { \frac { c + d T } { a + b T } } \right) .
$$

so that the eigenvalues of $\mathrm { d } \tilde { f } _ { M }$ at $q _ { \alpha }$ are $\alpha ^ { 3 }$ and $\alpha ^ { - 2 }$ , which are different from 1.

We can now apply the Lefschetz fixed point formula.
As $\mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( X , \mathbb { R } ) = \mathrm { H } ^ { 3 } ( X , \mathbb { R } ) = 0$ , we get that the number of fixed points of $\varphi _ { M }$ is equal to Tr $\widetilde { f } _ { M | \mathrm { H } ^ { 2 } ( X , \mathbb { R } ) } ^ { * } + 2$ .
Then we can use formula (4.2): the trace on the part $\left( \land _ { \mathbb { Z } } ^ { 2 } ( \Lambda ^ { * } \times \Lambda ^ { * } ) \right) ^ { G } \otimes _ { \mathbb { Z } } \mathbb { R }$ is the sum

$$
| \alpha | ^ { 2 } + | \beta | ^ { 2 } + \alpha \overline { { { \beta } } } + \overline { { { \alpha } } } \beta = | \alpha + \beta | ^ { 2 } ;
$$

and since $M$ belongs to $H$ , $\varphi _ { M }$ acts trivially on the factor $\textstyle \bigoplus _ { i = 1 } ^ { 9 } \mathbb { R } [ \widetilde { E } _ { i } ]$ so that the trace on this factor ϕis 9. This gives the required result.


We will now study in greater details the set $\Theta _ { 1 }$ .
Let us define three subsets $S _ { 1 } , S _ { 2 } , S _ { 3 }$ of $A$ as follows:

$$
\sharp \wedge S \enspace \mid f _ { M } ( p ) = p \} , S _ { 2 } = \{ p \in A \setminus S \enspace \mid f _ { M } ( p ) = \phi ( p ) \} , S _ { 3 } = \{ p \in A \setminus S \enspace \mid f _ { M } ( p ) = \phi ^ { 2 } ( p ) \} .
$$

To compute the cardinality of the $S _ { i }$ ’s, we use the following statement:

Lemma 4.13. For any matrix $P$ in ${ \mathrm { G L } } ( 2 ; \Lambda )$ such that 1 is not an eigenvalue of $P$ , the automorphism fP of the complex torus A has $\left| 1 - \operatorname { T r } ( P ) + \operatorname * { d e t } P \right| ^ { 2 }$ fixed points.

Proof.
Since the fixed points of $f _ { P }$ are non-degenerate, we can apply the holomorphic Lefschetz fixed point formula ([23, p. 426], [39], [12]); this gives

$$
1 - { \overline { { \operatorname { T r } ( P ) } } } + { \overline { { \operatorname* { d e t } P } } } = \# \operatorname { F i x } \left( f _ { P } \right) \times { \frac { 1 } { 1 - \operatorname { T r } \left( P \right) + \operatorname { d e t } P } } .
$$

Corollary 4.14. Let M be a matrix in $H$ such that $M ^ { 3 } \neq \mathrm { i d } .$ .
One has the following equalities:

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \# S _ { 1 } = \left| \operatorname { T r } \left( M \right) - 2 \right| ^ { 2 } - 9 \qquad \quad a n d \qquad \# S _ { 2 } = \# S _ { 3 } = \left| \operatorname { T r } \left( M \right) + 1 \right| ^ { 2 } - 9 . } \end{array}
$$

Proof.
This follows directly from Lemma 4.13, since

$$
S _ { 1 } = \operatorname { F i x } { ( f _ { M } ) } \setminus S , \qquad S _ { 2 } = \operatorname { F i x } { ( f _ { \mathrm { j } ^ { 2 } M } ) } \setminus S \qquad { \mathrm { ~ a n d ~ } } \qquad S _ { 3 } = \operatorname { F i x } { ( f _ { \mathrm { j } M } ) } \setminus S .
$$

The group $G$ acts transitively on each set $S _ { i }$ .
Then $\Theta _ { 1 }$ can be written as the disjoint union of the quotients $S _ { i } / G$ .
Remark that we can obtain in this way the result of Proposition 4.12: indeed, if $t = \operatorname { T r } ( M )$ ,

$$
\# \Theta _ { 1 } = \frac { \left| t - 2 \right| ^ { 2 } } { 3 } - 3 + 2 \left( \frac { \left| 1 + t \right| ^ { 2 } } { 3 } - 3 \right) = \left| t \right| ^ { 2 } - 7
$$

Since # $\Theta _ { 2 } = 1 8$ , this gives # $\mathrm { F i x } \left( \varphi _ { M } \right) = \left| t \right| ^ { 2 } + 1 1$ .

As $\mathrm { H } ^ { 0 } ( X , \mathrm { T } X ) = \mathrm { H } ^ { 2 } ( X , \mathrm { T } X ) = \{ 0 \}$ , the holomorphic Atiyah-Bott fixed point formula [1, Thm. 4.12] , ,yields the following result :

Proposition $4 . 1 5 . \ T h e t r a c e \ o f ( \varphi _ { M } ^ { - 1 } ) _ { * } a c t i n g \ o n \ H ^ { 1 } ( X , \mathrm { T } X ) i s \ e q u a l \ t o \ t h e s u m - \sum _ { x } \frac { \mathrm { T r \ } \mathrm { d } ( \varphi _ { M } ^ { - 1 } ) _ { x } } { \operatorname* { d e t } ( \mathrm { i d } - \mathrm { d } ( \varphi _ { M } ) _ { x } ) }$ where $x$ runs through the fixed points of $\varphi _ { M }$ .

Corollary 4.16. Let M be a matrix in $H$ such that $M ^ { 3 } \ne$ id.
Then the trace of $( \varphi _ { M } ^ { - 1 } ) _ { * }$ acting on $\mathrm { H } ^ { 1 } ( X , \mathrm { T } X )$ is $8 \operatorname { T r } ( M )$ .

Proof.
We divide the fixed point set of $\varphi _ { M }$ in four parts: $S _ { 1 } / G , S _ { 2 } / G , S _ { 3 } / G$ and $\Theta _ { 2 }$ .
The first three parts correspond to $\Theta _ { 1 }$ ϕ / /.
We start by computing the contribution of $\Theta _ { 2 }$ /in the sum of Proposition 4.15. Let us put $t = \operatorname { T r } \left( M \right) = \alpha + \alpha ^ { - 1 }$ .
Any pair of fixed points in a divisor $E _ { i }$ yields the term

$$
\frac { \alpha ^ { - 3 } + \alpha ^ { 2 } } { ( 1 - \alpha ^ { 3 } ) ( 1 - \alpha ^ { - 2 } ) } + \frac { \alpha ^ { 3 } + \alpha ^ { - 2 } } { ( 1 - \alpha ^ { - 3 } ) ( 1 - \alpha ^ { 2 } ) }
$$

so that the contribution of $\Theta _ { 2 }$ is $- 9 t - { \frac { 6 } { t - 2 } } - { \frac { 3 } { 1 + t } } \cdot$ Now the contributions of $S _ { 1 } / G , S _ { 2 } / G$ and $S _ { 3 } / G$ are respectively

$$
\frac { \varkappa ^ { 2 } | t - 2 | ^ { 2 } } { 3 } - 3 ) \frac { t } { 2 - t } , \qquad ( \frac { | 1 + t | ^ { 2 } } { 3 } - 3 ) \frac { - \mathbf { j } ^ { 2 } t } { 1 + t } \qquad \mathrm { a n d } \qquad ( \frac { | 1 + t | ^ { 2 } } { 3 } - 3 ) \frac { - \mathbf { j } t } { 1 + t }
$$

so that the contribution of $\Theta _ { 1 }$ is $t + \frac { 3 t } { t - 2 } - \frac { 3 t } { 1 + t } .$ Adding the contributions of $\Theta _ { 1 }$ and $\Theta _ { 2 }$ we get  
$- 8 t$ .

4.2.2. Second proof by sheaf theory.
We start by fixing some conventions and notations that are specific to this section.
For any group $G$ a $G$ -module will mean a right $G$ -module, that is a contravariant representation of $G$ on some vector space.
We denote the vector space $\mathbb { C } ^ { 2 }$ by $V$ .
The same convention goes for equivariant vector bundles: they will all be right equivariant $( s e e \ \ S 3 . 2 . 1 )$ .
If nothing else is specified, we consider $V$ as a natural right ${ \mathrm { G L } } ( 2 ; \Lambda )$ -module, where a matrix $M$ acts by $M ^ { - 1 }$ .
We start by the two following exact sequences of sheaves:

First exact sequence of sheaves on $\widetilde { \mathcal { A } }$ relating TA and TAe via the blowup map

$$
0 \longrightarrow \mathrm { T } \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } \longrightarrow \delta ^ { * } \mathrm { T } \mathcal { A } \longrightarrow \bigoplus _ { i = 1 } ^ { 9 } \iota _ { E _ { i } * } \mathrm { T } _ { \widetilde { E _ { i } } } ( - 1 ) \longrightarrow 0 .
$$

This sequence is ${ \mathrm { G L } } ( 2 ; \Lambda )$ -equivariant and $G$ -equivariant.
Let us explain its construction: the first map is the differential of the map $\delta \colon { \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } }  { \mathcal { A } }$ corresponding to the blowup of the nine points in $S$ ; δas a sheaf morphism it is injective.
For any $i$ with $1 \leq i \leq 9$ and any point $[ \ell ]$ in $E _ { i }$ (corresponding

to a line $\ell$ in $\mathrm { T } _ { p _ { i } } \mathcal { R } ,$ ), the image of $( \delta _ { * } ) _ { [ \ell ] } \colon \mathrm { T } _ { [ \ell ] }  { \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } } \to \mathrm { T } _ { p _ { i } }  { \mathcal { A } }$ is precisely the line $\ell$ .
Thanks to the Euler \`exact sequence [32, Prop. 2.4.4]

$$
0 \longrightarrow O _ { E _ { i } } ( - 1 ) \longrightarrow \mathrm { T } _ { p _ { i } } \mathscr { A } \otimes O _ { E _ { i } } \longrightarrow \mathrm { T } E _ { i } ( - 1 ) \longrightarrow 0
$$

the complex line $\frac { \mathrm { T } _ { p _ { i } } \mathcal { A } } { \mathrm { I m } \left( \delta _ { * } \right) _ { [ \ell ] } }$ identifies canonically with the fiber of $\mathrm { T } _ { \widetilde { E } _ { i } } ( - 1 )$ at $[ \ell ]$ .

Remark that $\mathrm { T } _ { \widetilde { E } _ { i } } ( - 1 )$ is isomorphic to $O _ { E _ { i } } ( 1 )$ , but this isomorphism is not canonical and cannot be made compatible in any way with the action of ${ \mathrm { G L } } ( 2 ; \Lambda )$ .

Second exact sequence of sheaves on $X$ relating $\operatorname { T } \widetilde { \mathcal { A } }$ and TX via the cyclic cover

$$
0 \longrightarrow \pi _ { * } ( \mathrm { T } \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } ) ^ { G } \longrightarrow \mathrm { T } X \longrightarrow \bigoplus _ { i = 1 } ^ { 9 } \iota _ { E _ { i } * } \mathrm { N } _ { E _ { i } / X } \longrightarrow 0 .
$$

This sequence is ${ \mathrm { G L } } ( 2 ; \Lambda )$ -equivariant.
Let us again explain its construction: for any open subset $U$ of $X$ , the sections of the sheaf $\pi _ { * } ( \mathrm { T } \widetilde { \mathcal { A } } ) ^ { G }$ on $U$ are exactly the $G$ -invariant holomorphic vector fields on $\pi ^ { - 1 } ( U )$ π.
